Preface
This thesis has been prepared over six months at the Department of Civil Engineering,
at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfilment for the degree Master of
Science in Civil Engineering, MSc Civil Eng.
The project is part of a cluster project regarding the construction of a cover to a part of the
Elsinore freeway close to Copenhagen. This thesis regards, in particular, the construction
of a concrete dome on the covered area, to be used for commercial or leisure purposes.
The project is based on the use of Super-Light concrete and Pearl-Chain technology.

iv
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
This project is part of a cluster project concerning the design of a concrete construction
to cover a stretch of the Elsinore freeway as it approaches Copenhagen. The cover will
serve the neighbourhood as a green area directed towards leisure and commercial ac-
tivities, whilst reducing the inconveniences linked to the proximity of the area to traffic
circulation. This Master thesis focuses on the design of a dome concrete structure lo-
cated in the surface of the covered area, with the purpose of hosting a market or other
activities that may improve the daily life of the citizens.
The design of the structure is based on Pearl-Chain (PC) an Super-Light (SL) technol-
ogy. The main approach is to explore aesthetically appealing architectural shapes, which
usually result in inefficient and material-consuming constructions, and apply innovative
technologies, like the previously mentioned, to achieve greener, lighter and more efficient
designs.
The proposal subject of this project consists of two twin spherical dome structures created
from multiple pre-cast trapezoidal elements comprising both regular and light aggregate
concrete. These elements are post-tensioned together in radial portions of the domes be-
fore being erected and positioned against each other endowing the domes with its curved
nature. High priority is given to the comfort of the users in the design and therefore, the
creation of an open space, with plenty of light, lead to a glass lantern at the top of each
dome and glass side parapets interrupting the domes as inclined planes cutting into them
and giving each of them a triangular shape in plan view. The sudden interruption of the
domes requires of structures to support the precast trapezoidal elements placed above.
To solve this issue, the domes are provided with Pearl-Chain arches at the cut edges.
These edge arches will consist of straight beam-like segments in regular concrete that
will be post-tensioned together forming an arch unit before being erected to their inclined
position. This leaves each dome to be supported at three points, which are placed at
each side of the freeway, with the domes spanning 32 m, to avoid introducing large un-
favourable loads into the structure providing a cover for the freeway.
This report incorporates preliminary design brainstorming, the verification of the super-
structure in the chosen design, a brief proposal of the constructive process and an assess-
ment of the sustainable aspect of the structure, including a comparison of CO2 emissions
in similar constructions.

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:13==
1
Introduction
Concrete domes have been built since the roman period, with the Pantheon being one of
the main landmarks in the history of concrete domes. Since then, the construction of this
type of structures has declined due to its complex and expensive construction, involving
in-situ casting with temporary formwork structures.
Nowadays, there is a global tendency in construction seeking high degrees of precast el-
ements due to the high demand of time and cost effectiveness. The use of these precast
elements, comes with inherent hindering to architectural freedom. Creating interesting
shapes and forms from precast concrete elements results in less efficient load carrying
structures that require heavier cross-sections, ruining the aesthetically pleasing visuals of
a slender concrete dome.
New technologies, such as Super-Light concrete, can make the structure significantly
lighter. This technology was invented by Kristian D. Hertz in 2008, and is based on the
principle of using a strong concrete, where the compression forces should be in the cross-
section of a structural element and filling out the rest of the cross-section of with a light
aggregate concrete [1]. This significantly reduces the total weight of the structure and
as a consequence, CO2 emmisions. Paired with Pearl-Chain reinforcement, this technol-
ogy can provide an efficient approach which is both time and material saving, creating
a pathway towards a more sustainable construction. The aim of this Master thesis is to
apply these innovative methods and materials to a practical design of a concrete dome
construction, focusing on unleashing the freedom of complex geometrical elements in the
design and achieving a light and sustainable solution.
1.1
Background
The Elsinore freeway crosses the Ryparken-Lundehus district in Copenhagen. The area
is one of the greenest, most peaceful areas in Copenhagen, but the neighbourhood suf-
fers from the nuissances rooted by the close traffic circulation, including air and noise
pollution and most importantly, the division of the neighbourhood by the freeway. This
project is part of a cluster project motivated by the desire to reconnect the sides of the
neighbourhood that have been divided and creating a green urban area dedicated to the
leisure and service supply of the Ryparken-Lundehus citizens.
To remove the inconveniences caused by traffic along the freeway, a concrete construc-
tion will be built covering a stretch of the highway, which runs in a ditch. The area on the
surface of this cover will be equipped with nature and facilities so as to create an green
urban landscape that evades the neighbourhood from the underlying traffic hustle. The
object of this part of the cluster project is to provide the neighbourhood with a concrete
dome construction that will serve as a market square, as well as becoming a landmark of
the district announcing a green welcome to Copenhagen.
A plan view of the area showing the proposed stretch of freeway to be covered and the

1.2
Scope and limitations
The aim of this project is to deliver a preliminary design of a concrete dome structure using
Pearl-Chain reinforcement and Super-Light concrete. The topics included range from the
initial ideas for the geometrical design to a final design and construction process proposal.
This thesis is organized into chapters that have been organized to mimic the order of
the tasks in the design process that has been followed, in order to give an accurate rep-
resentation of the real chronological order of the ideas collected in this report.
• Chapter 2 discusses the initial ideas for the geometry of the dome and justifies the
thoughts that lead to the chosen geometry. Each of the elements that will consti-
tute the dome structure is described and their basic dimensions are provided. This
chapter also includes the materials that will be used for each of these elements.
• Chapter 3 focuses on the illustration of the load paths along the structure and pro-
vides a reasoning behind the design approach used for each of the main elements
of the dome structure.
• Chapter 4 mentions the different loads to be considered in the design and provides
the calculations and data that resulted in the load values used in the design. It also
states the load combinations that should be used in different limit states.
• Chapter 5 aims to obtain the axial forces in the top cupula rings and describes the
procedure employed to do this. This chapter also describes the process that lead to
the final cross-section design of the rings, which is designed to withstand the axial
forces obtained previously.
• Chapter 6 describes the obtention of the design sectional forces via a simple finite el-
ement model of the radial arches and explains the process of the final cross-section
design and the optimization process of the cross-section dimensions in these ele-
ments according to these forces.
• Chapter 7 is dedicated to analyzing the sectional forces in the edge arch with a 3D
finite element model and discusses the final cross-section required to resist them
whilst optimizing the design with eccentricities in the prestress.
• Chapter 8 is dedicated to other details relevant to design, including estimation of the
dimensions of other structural elements not mentioned in the previous chapters and
suggestions for some connections and constructive details that will not be analysed
in this thesis.
• Chapter 9 proposes an idea for the sequential steps in the construction process of
the structure and describes any temporary supports needed during construction.
• Chapter 10 evaluates the sustainability of the final design, by assessing the CO2
emmissions from its production and construction, providing a comparison to the
emmissions from a regular in-situ concrete dome with the same spherical radius.
It must be noted that this project is a basic design and it focuses on the estimation of
the cross-section characteristics of the main structural elements comprised in the super-
structure. Further necessary in-depth calculations and analysis are required to develop
the design into a detailed design. Some further development is suggested in Chapter 11:
Conclusions and further development.

==PAGE:17==
2
Geometry and materials
This chapter will follow the process of defining the geometry of the structure through the
initial brainstorming ideas to the detailed description of each element in the final design,
including the materials used for each of the parts.
2.1
Initial requirements and brainstorming
The urban area to be created on the surface of the freeway cover has to have a green
motif and become a landmark for the Ryparken-Lundehus district. Curved shaped struc-
tures catch the eye of the viewer and are easily recognisable because of the contrast
with the surrounding building blocks, therefore they are prone to becoming a symbol of
the neighbourhood. In this project, priority has been given to freedom in the geometry,
hence, different curved-shaped domes where explored.
The main design requirement in this project is the need for the structure to span the 32m
width of the freeway to avoid introducing unwanted large point loads into the arch struc-
ture below. The width of the highways was measured a few meters away from Emdrupvej
bridge (shown in Figure 2.1).
Figure 2.1: Measured width of the freeway. Source: Google Maps.
Also, the height of the structure was limited to around the height of the neighbouring build-
ings (around 10 m) as a very high structure would block the views of the surrounding build-
ings and close up the space, projecting shadows that would create a darker atmosphere.
This the opposite from the objective of this design as the aim is to create an open space,
integrated into the surroundings and very diaphanous, letting in as much light as possible.
After exploring several shapes, the three alternatives in Figure 2.2 were considered.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
5
==PAGE:18==
Figure 2.2: Alternative ideas for the geometry of the structure.
The first one, a spherical dome with 4 supports and cuts along the edges where the side
walls made from glass will begin. The idea of letting more light on the inside with some
glass elements in the roof was explored. The second alternative is another spherical
dome with 3 supports instead. The same idea of glass walls is included, this time inclined
towards the inside to try to follow the shape of the sphere and also to get even more light
inside. The last alternative explored is a hyperbolic shape. In this case, the arches form-
ing the ribs would be parabolic, with varying height. The ones in the edges are inclined
outwards, with the end arch cantilevered over the highway, symbolising a gate to the city.
Initially alternative 3 was discarded because the parabolic arches would require more
height and they would eclipse the surroundings. Between the other two alternatives, the
second one was preferred, mainly due to the non-aligned walls to the path of the freeway
below, breaking down this alignment gives the structure a very different look depending
on the perspective and this aspect results very interesting as the structure will be more
appealing from all the possible viewpoints in its surroundings. However, in making an
equilateral triangle shape in plan view, it was discovered that the area covered by the
dome may not be enough to host a very busy activity such as a market. Therefore, a
fourth alternative was set up, based on the 3 point supported dome, by adding a twin
dome increasing the covered area but maintaining the desired aspects described above.
6
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:19==
Figure 2.3: Final alternative for the geometry of the structure.
A lantern was added at the top to increase the incoming light. whilst the space between
the two domes is also made in glass for this same purpose. The spherical shape was
chosen due to the simplicity in the division of the Pearl-Chain dome into its elementary
units.
2.2
Final design
Once the initial shape is found, it is applied to the site conditions, where the supports have
to span the width of the freeway. When observed from a plan view, the domes look like
equilateral triangles with one support on one side of the freeway and two on the other.
This means the height of the equilateral triangle is 32m.
The height of the dome is set to 10m, to approximately match the surrounding buildings.
These geometrical restrictions result in a spherical surface with radius:
R = 42.67m
(2.1)
The planes cutting the edges of the twin domed are inclined 12.67º towards the center
of each of the domes. This makes the horizontal radius at the top point of the cut to be
17.33m, this will constitute the top cupula, which will be analysed separately (see Chapter
3).
The lantern is chosen to have a 4m diameter.
2.2.1
Pearl-Chain elementary units
In order to apply Pearl-Chain technology, the dome has to be divided into the different
precast elementary units that will be prestressed together in a pearl-chain manner, join-
ing them together forming larger units.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
7
==PAGE:20==
Taking into account the size limitations to allow the elements to be transported to the
site, the dome was divided radially into 18 pieces, cut by vertical planes every 20º. Hori-
zontally, 6 planes were used to cut the dome into smaller pieces. One of the planes will
cut through the top points of the cut edges, making the top cupula division. The top and
bottom parts of this cut will also be divided by two more horizontal planes to reduce the
size of the elements.
The result of these divisions is shown in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Element types on one of the twin domes.
The trapezoidal elements resulting from the division are classified into types according to
their dimensions and their correspondence in the FE-model analysis in Chapter 6. Spe-
cial trapezoidal elements with skew edges are found where the surface of the dome is
cut making the triangular shape. Elements T3SE and T11SE are some of these elements,
which are not numbered by themselves because they are treated as T3 and T11 elements
in the calculations.
These trapezoidal elements will include the Super-Light component that characterizes
this design. They will be precasted with perimeter beams in regular 55MPa concrete and
a light aggregate concrete will be poured as filling. A sketch of the constitutive parts and
materials of one trapezoidal element is shown in Figure 2.5 and the properties of the
materials used can be found in Section 2.5.
8
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:21==
Figure 2.5: Edge beams and filling in a representative trapezoidal Pearl-Chain element
The dimensions of the trapezoidal elements are discussed in the chapters dedicated to
the design.
2.3
Edge arches
The cuts making the triangular shape from plan view require another structural element to
be added to the design. The purpose of this element is to receive the loads from the ele-
ments which are not directly supported due to the cut. A regular concrete arch, also made
from Pearl-Chain elements prestressed together, but this time, made only from 55MPa
concrete.
This arch will be inclined 12.67º, following the shape of the cut. The span from support to
support is 34.15m. More details on the edge arch can be found in Chapter 7.
2.4
Other features
The open spaces in the domes (lantern, inclined walls and gap between domes) will all
be covered by glass as shown in Figure 2.6.
The angles surface resulting from the trapezoidal elements placed together will be smoothed
out with a green roof, matching the theme of the main idea of this project and creating the
idea of a camouflage cover on the dome, mimicking the surrounding areas which are also
to be converted into green areas.
A rendered image of the final design is shown in Figure 2.6. More captured angles can
be found in Annex F.

2.5.3
Nominal cover of reinforcement
According to Section 4.2 and Table 4.1 in [4], exposure class should be XF3, correspond-
ing to horizontal concrete surfaces exposed to rain and freezing. This exposure class is
included in Environmental class “Aggressive” in Table 4.1 in [3].
According to the same source, [3], Tables 4.4N and 4.5N, for non-prestressing Steel, mini-
mum concrete cover is Cmin,dur=30mm, whilst for post-tensioned tendons it is 40mm. The
chosen allowance in design for deviation is the minimum, ∆cdev=5mm (Section 4.4.1.3 in
[3]). This is suitable for normal and extended inspection levels which is the case for pre-
cast concrete elements.
Therefore the resulting nominal covers are:
• Cnom,s=Cmin+∆cdev=30+5=35mm for mild reinforcement
• Cnom,p=Cmin+∆cdev=40+5=45mm for pre-stress tendons
However, Table 4.2 in [4] states that the minimum concrete cover for prestressing tendons
is the diameter of the duct. The maximum value between both values will be used in the
design.

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:25==
3
Static system and design approach
This chapter is entirely dedicated to the understanding of the structure and the paths loads
follow as they are transmitted along the structure to the foundations. The complexity of
the dome structure being analysed makes this an essential subject to discuss as the load
paths define the design approach.
3.1
Static system
The structural behaviour of the dome is illustrated by describing the load paths in a uniform
load case scenario, e.g. dead load. When the dome receives the load from uniform load-
ing, the light aggregate concrete filling in the trapezoidal elements will transfer the load
in shear, distributing the load onto the perimeter regular concrete beams of these same
elements. The side edge beams of the element will develop meridional forces travelling
along it, similarly to arch structures. These forces will be compression forces and will in-
crease as they flow towards the foundations. Bending moments are usually insignificant
for uniform loads as the spherical shape is very efficient in carrying uniform loading, par-
ticularly in low-span ranges. The deviation of the structure being analysed from a perfectly
symmetrical dome, with supports all around its base, will give rise to significant bending
moments in these side edge beams, that will be addressed in the design, with the aid of
Pearl-Chain reinforcement.
The particular distinction of a dome structure with respect to arch structures is their ca-
pacity to resist some of the bending forces on the meridians by means of axial forces
developing in the horizontal ring structures, formed by the top and bottom beams of the
trapezoidal elements in this case. These rings will unload some of the meridional arch-
type structures, permitting the reach of longer spans with lighter cross-sections. Looking
at the top of the dome, when loaded by dead load, the arch-type structures will deflect
downwards, pushing the trapezoidal elements together and therefore, activating the ring
action of the top ring as compression. When moving further down along the meridional
arches if the span is too large, then the weight of the structure above will induce large
downwards deformations at midspan producing outwards deformations at lower points
on the arch. These deformations may then be taken by ring forces, holding the arches
at this level and impeding their outwards expansion. As a consequence, tension may
eventually develop in the rings as the span increases. The lowest ring will take all the
tension from the loads on the dome, preventing the arches from expanding outwards at
their supports. The bottom ring, working in tension, will also prevent the transmission of
horizontal forces onto the supporting structure.
The structural behaviour described above the principal of load paths along a theoretical
spherical dome. The spherical shape is in this case cut with 3 inclined planes, interrupting
the natural flow of forces along the dome and diverting them along other paths. To avoid
undesirable bending moments and tension because of the cut lower rings, the dome is
divided into two parts: a top cupula with full meridional-hoop interaction to resist forces
and three narrowing ”leg-type” structures towards the supports (see Figure 3.1). The load
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
13
==PAGE:26==
paths flow along the top cupula as described in the previous paragraphs, until it reaches
the bottom ring of the cupula, which will be designed in order to absorb all the tension from
the trapezoidal elements above this point, meaning no horizontal forces are transmitted
from the cupula to the legs. The vertical forces flow downwards along the radial arches
until they reach the cut. At this point the arches need to be supported and consequently,
a two-hinge edge arch has been necessary to receive these mainly vertical loads from
the radial arches.
Figure 3.1: Top cupula and leg supports
The radial arches introduce large point loads on the edge arches, and this is not a situation
where the arch shape is proven to be effective. However, the prestress of the edge arch
and, more importantly in this case, the position of the prestress in the section, can give the
cross-section of the arch a more optimized design. The horizontal forces applied to the
edge arch are derived from the trapezoidal elements situated below the lower cupula ring
and supported on the edge arch. This is only very few elements, transmitting very small
horizontal loads. However, these loads can result very critical as they compromise the
stability of the whole system. A horizontal force outwards on the edge arch can cause the
arch to tilt and collapse as it is hinged at supports. To compensate this, the edge arches
have been placed at an angle, tilted inwards, with its own self-weight and the vertical point
loads from the radial arches acting to compensate this effect. This also compresses the
trapezoidal elements in the legs together, contributing to avoid tension in this part of the
structure. The inclined edge arches resist the loads in compression forces and biaxial
bending towards the supports, onto the foundations.
3.2
Design approach
The paths followed by loads acting on the structure define the design approach of each
of the elements.
14
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:27==
The top cupula is analyzed separately, obtaining the ring forces that appear in the rings,
used in the design of these elements.
However, the effects of the rings are not taken into account when designing the merid-
ional arches. This is due to the fact that not all elements in the arch benefit from this
additional resistance because of the inclined cuts in the dome, and therefore, a conser-
vative approach, assuming no contribution from the hoop effect has been used to design
the radial arches. Furthermore, the arches have been designed as three-hinged arches
because of the lantern at the top of the cupula and the uncertainty of how much bending
moment can be transmitted by the interaction of one arch in its connection to the rest.
The three-hinged arch approach also constitutes a solution on the safe side, resulting in
larger sectional forces used in the design of the radial arches.
The edge arches are designed as two-hinged arches taking the loads from the 5 radial
arches to which they provide support.
The horizontal ribs between edge arches resulting from the cut lower rings, will be anal-
ysed separately combining the action of the loads on the dome and the action of the edge
arches reclined on them. These elements are the top and bottom edge beams of trape-
zoidal elements T1 and T2 (see Figure 2.4.
The chapters addressing the design of each of the elements mentioned are:
• Chapter 5 for the design of the cupula rings.
• Chapter 6 for the design of the radial/meridional arches.
• Chapter 7 for the design of the inclined edge arches.
• Chapter 8 for the design of the top and bottom edge beams in trapezoidal elements
T1 and T2.
It must be noted that both domes are exactly the same and therefore, the design of the
elements is focused on only one of the twin domes.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
15
==PAGE:28==
16
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:29==
4
Loads
This chapter deals with the calculations required to obtain load values as well as the load
combinations used in the structure’s verification.
The loads considered to act on the dome are: the self-weight of the structure, dead load
from non-structural elements, imposed loads, wind loads and snow loads.
Aside from the values for the loads, this chapter will also explain the distribution of these
loads on the structure. The forces developed due to load application to each element will
be dealt in Chapters 5, 6 and 7.
4.1
Permanent loads
4.1.1
Self-weight of the structure
For application of the loads, the self-weight has been subdivided into:
• Weight of the edge beams - the width of the edge beams varies linearly along the
elements, so their weight is modelled as a trapezoidal distributed load (qedge,t at the
top and qedge,b at the bottom) along each linear element of the 2D model (shown in
Figure 6.2).
• Weight corresponding to the light concrete filling - applied as a trapezoidal dis-
tributed load (with values qlight,t at the top and qlight,b at the bottom) varying linearly
along the whole length of the element.
• Weight from the top and bottom beams in each element - Resulting in point loads
applied at nodes in the model (Qtop and Qbottom). The resulting point load applied is
the value of the weight of the strong concrete top or bottom beams minus the weight
already considered by the trapezoidal distributed load.

4.2
Variable loads
4.2.1
Imposed load on roof
The inclination of the dome at ground level hinders the possible access of it to pedestrians
and consequently, the roof is category H according to Section 6.3.4.1 in [6].
Despite this, minimum characteristic values for imposed loads have to be considered to
account for loads that may be derive from maintenance and repair activities. The values
for the loads can be found in [7] 6.3.4.2, where it is stated that a concentrated load, Qk,
of 1.5 kN must be applied.
Several load cases need to be considered, with different positions for this load, to de-
termine the most critical situation for design. This issue is discussed in Chapter 6 under
the relevant subsection.
4.2.2
Wind load
Wind loads are calculated following the guidelines in [8].
Peak velocity pressure
The fundamental value of the basic wind velocity, vb,0, is given in the national annex [9],
and is equal to 24m/s. In [8] section 4.2 basic wind velocity is defined as:
vb = CdirCseasonvb,0
(4.22)
Where Cdir and Cseason are directional and seasonal factors, which are both taken as 1.
Mean wind velocity is calculated as:
vm(z) = cr(z)co(z)vb
(4.23)
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
21
==PAGE:34==
cr(z) is an orography factor, chosen as 1 and cr(z) depends on terrain roughness, which
is calculated according to [8] section 4.3.2 and has a value of 0.54 for chosen Terrain
Category IV and height of z=10m (highest point of the structure above ground).
With these factors, mean wind velocity has a calculated value of 12.95 m/s.
The turbulence intensity at height z is calculated as in [8] Section 4.4:
Iv(z) =
σv

(4.24)

(4.26)
The roughness length, z0, is equal to 1 as stated in Table 4.1 in [8] for terrain category IV,
corresponding to an area in which at least 15% of the surface is covered with buildings.
The value for zo,II is fixed and equal to 0.05m, and therefore, kr=0.234.
Peak velocity pressure, qp(z), at the maximum height of the dome (10m) is then (Sec-
tion 4.5 in [8]):
qp(z) = [1 + 7Iv(z)]0.5ρvm(z)2
(4.27)
Air density ρ is set to 1,23 kg/m3, resulting in a qp(10)=416.65Pa.
External pressure coefficients
Based on the peak velocity pressure, the pressure to be applied as wind load on each
of the dome’s trapezoidal portions can be found. The procedure is explained in [8] 7.2.8,
where 3 coefficients A (affecting one edge of the dome), B (for the highest point of the
dome) and C (at the other edge of the dome) are obtained from geometric characteristics
and Figure 4.6.

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:35==
Figure 4.6: Wind pressure coefficients from Figure 7.12 in [8].
The geometrical parameters have values of d=42.67m, h=0 and f= 10m for the dome
structure, and therefore the resulting coefficients are A=0.4, B=-0.64 and C=0.
These coefficients factorize the peak velocity pressure in the positions mentioned above
to obtain the wind pressure at each point of the dome. Lineal variation is used to interpo-
late values for the trapezoidal elements which are positioned in between these 3 known
points. Graphically, the trapezoidal elements are each assigned a pressure coefficient
according to the position of their centroid, as shown in Figure 4.6.

The wind pressure applied on each trapezoidal element is the result of the multiplication
of the pressure coefficient by the peak velocity pressure.
4.2.3
Snow load
The snow load to be considered has been obtained according to [10].
There are two load cases to consider: an undrifted load arrangement and a drifted load
arrangement. The undrifted load arrangement is considered to be less unfavourable and
therefore only the drifted snow load arrangement is considered.
Furthermore, the drifted load case is taken from [11], Figure 5.5.c, where a special case
is detailed for cylindrical structures with an initial angle β0 less than 60º. In the case of
the structure subject of this project, the angle at the ground is 50º, and therefore, the load
case to be used in the analysis of the structure is as shown in Figure 4.8.

5
Design of cupula rings
This section refers to the design of the cupula rings (R1, R2, R3 and R4). The purpose
of this chapter is to obtain the sectional forces in all of the rings and design their cross-
section accordingly.
Figure 5.1: Identification of cupula rings.
When subjected to uniform load, the cupula rings will most likely be in compression, how-
ever, this compression will decrease from the top to the bottom rings with the last ring (R4)
taking all the tension from the loads applied. This is the reason why this ring requires pre-
stress.
The bottom ring can be designed with the required prestress to ensure that all the horizon-
tal reactions coming from the top cupula can be taken by the ring and therefore transfer no
horizontal forces downwards to the rest of the structure. This is especially useful as these
forces would create unwanted tension in the triangular legs and undesired moments in
the edge arches.
The rest of the rings will be checked to not develop tension and their cross-section will be
designed to take the compression forces.
5.1
Assumptions and further studies
It must be noted that the method used to calculate section forces in the present section
assumes the rings will be affected by uniformly distributed loads, inducing only axial forces
in the rings. This does not reflect reality completely, as the horizontal forces will be mainly
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
29
==PAGE:42==
carried down by the radial arches to the ring and therefore, it is really point loads acting at
the positions where the radial arches meet the rings. This means bending moments will
actually appear in the rings.
The loading may also be different at each point load acting on the arch if some radial
arches are subjected to larger loads, and these asymmetries in the loading would also
produce moments in the rings. However, it is beyond the scope of this project to analyse
these moments and only the axial forces will be used in the design.
This study will provide approximate dimensions of the concrete cross-section for the rings
(the top and bottom strong concrete beams of each trapezoidal element) and an estima-
tion of the pre-stress force and therefore, number of pre-stressing strands required.
The prestress to be provided is assumed to have no eccentricity with respect to the cen-
troid of the concrete section for the sake of this study. However, the bending moments
that can arise from further detailed calculations of the forces in the rings can be taken by
adding eccentricities to the cable so that the moment caused by the prestress force can
counteract the bending moments from uneven loading on the rings. This eccentricity will
position the cable closer to the outer side of the ring cross-section at the points where
the radial arches meet the ring. This is both structurally efficient and geometrically re-
quired as at this points, the positioning of both the prestress in the cupula’s bottom ring
and the radial arches at the centroid of the section will cause a clash where both elements
meet. Taking advantage of the eccentricity in the ring can solve this problem. However,
as mentioned earlier, for the sake of the calculations leading to this pre-design of the cross
section of the ring, the prestress is assumed with no eccentricity.
5.2
Sectional forces in the cupula rings
This section explains the procedure to obtain the forces in the cupula rings (R1, R2, R3
and R4) as well as the resulting values of these forces for each load case. The purpose
of this section is to obtain the forces in all of the rings so they can later be designed to
remain in compression and uncracked in SLS and avoid brittle failure of the concrete in
ULS.
5.2.1
Procedure
Most of the loads considered have only a vertical component, e.g., structural and non-
structural dead loads, snow load and imposed load. The method consists in establishing
equilibrium in each of the trapezoidal elements composing the cupula. The trapezoidal
elements, TRi, in this chapter are named after the horizontal radius at the top of the el-
ement, Ri. The width of the trapezoidal element is Wi at the top, where the horizontal
radius is Ri, and width Wi+1 at the bottom, where the horizontal radius is Ri+1.
30
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:43==
Figure 5.2: Identification of trapezoidal elements in cupula.
Figure 5.3: Parameters for equilibrium of a trapezoidal element TRi.
The first step is to find the resultant vertical load acting upon the trapezoidal element (Pi).
This load is applied at the centroid of the element (ci). The Pi values for each load case
are specified in the following sections.
The ring forces at the top and at the bottom of the element required to achieve equilibrium
(Fi,top and Fi,bot) can be obtained by moment equilibrium at the bottom edge of each
element. A profile cut of the element shown in Figure 5.3, can be observed in Figure 5.4.
The horizontal forces Vi,top and Vi,bot correspond to the components of Fi, top and Fi, bot,
respectively, when projected onto the plane of the cut.

Figure 5.4: Profile cut of TRi in Figure 5.3.

Section forces due to wind load
The most critical situation is the case where the largest tension occurs in each of the rings.
Generally, wind pressure will add to the compression already caused by dead load in the
upper rings and add further tension to the bottom ring, R4. However, wind suction will
have the opposite effect and reduce compression in the upper rings while reducing the
tension in R4.
As explained in Section 4.2.2 and Figure 4.7, the whole cupula is subjected to wind suc-
tion, and therefore only this case is analysed, only with the aim of checking no tension
develops in the upper rings. However, in order to design the bottom ring, it is more criti-
cal to not consider this load case in load combinations as wind load will not increase the
tension in the bottom ring. Hence, the upper rings are checked to remain in tension by
calculating maximum wind suction load occurring in the cupula.
It is unlikely that the wind load on the cupula is the same all around it, but it is safe to
assume the maximum wind pressure and maximum suction on all radial portions for de-
sign purposes. As shown on Figure 4.7, the maximum wind suction occurring in elements
TR1, TR2 and TR3 has pressure coefficients of -0.6 occurring in all of these elements or
the whole of the radial portion of the cupula.
The wind load on the lantern is calculated as:
P0 = −0.6qpπR2
1
18
= −0.17kN
(5.15)
Where qp is the peak velocity pressure found in Section 4.2.2 and 18 is the number of
elements forming the top ring.
The total wind load Pi on each element is:
Pi = −0.6qpAi
(5.16)
Wind load, as a pressure load, has a horizontal component that needs to be take into
account by using Equations 5.4, 5.5, 5.6 and 5.7.

As discussed in Section 4.2.1, the imposed load Qk derived from maintenance and repair

There are many possible points of application of this load on the cupula, and as a conse-

ring and maximum tension in the bottom ring.

• Maximum compression in R1, occurs in the critical load case mentioned above

• Maximum compression in R2 is produced when Qk is applied on TR2 (Load Case
2).
• Maximum compression in R3 is produced when Qk is applied on TR3 (Load Case
3).

5.3
Design of the cupula rings
Rings R1, R2 and R3 are always in compression and therefore, no reinforcement is nec-
essary. Their cross-section is designed with the required area to withstand the maximum
compression in each of them.
The height of the section is equal to the height of the radial arches so that there is no
height difference between the edge beam of one same trapezoidal element. Thus, the
height of the section is fixed to h=440mm.
The required area is:
Areq = NEd/fcd
(5.18)
Where NEd is the design axial force, compression in this case, and fcd is the design com-
pressive resistance of the concrete (see Table 2.1. The values for required area in these
3 rings are very low. A minimum width of 10cm is given to the top and bottom edge beams
to ensure a good connection surface between the trapezoidal elements and this is more
restrictive than the required area.
R1 is composed by only the top beam in TR1 (T6 or T9 in Figure 2.4), therefore a 440x100mm
is provided. R2 is formed by the bottom beam in TR1 and the top beam in TR2. Both of
these beams have the minimum width mentioned, hence, the total section dimensions are
440x200mm. This is the same section used for R3, whose section is composed by the
bottom beam in TR2 and the top beam in TR3.
The design of the bottom ring, R4, differs from the previous rings. The ring is subjected to
tension and therefore, prestress reinforcement is needed to prevent cracking. Usually the
prestress provided would be to prevent cracks in SLS and mild reinforcement would be
added to resist the additional tension in ULS. However, in this case the tension values are
38
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:51==
very close (670.55 kN is SLS and 793.23 kN in ULS) and therefore prestress is designed
to avoid cracks in ULS, being on the safe side.
The pre-stressing of the ring establishes very particular conditions as the beams in the
trapezoidal elements have very small sections and would require very heavily reinforced
cross-sections with increased concrete dimensions to anchor with regular anchoring. A
specific solution to this problem was found in ME couplers from DYWIDAG [13]. These
couplers, also known as floating anchorage blocks, are specifically made to be applied
in rotation symmetric structures, like domes, where circumferential post-tensioning is re-
quired. The system is based on the belt-buckle principle and it consists of an anchoring
device with holes where the strands can be pulled through, overlapping each other. The
strands are then anchored on both sides of the anchoring block. Figure 5.6 illustrates this
prestressing system.
Figure 5.6: ME coupler used in the anchoring of pre-stress in R4. Source: DYWIDAG [13].
As mentioned in the assumptions, bending moments are not considered, and therefore
the prestress force, P, must be equal to the tension force, NEd.
The prestress losses are estimated to be 15% for long term losses and 15% for short
term losses. The short term losses are estimated higher than in the radial arches or the
edge arches due to the type of prestress used in the ring, which has a larger anchor slip
due to the previously mentioned particularities of the anchoring.
The required jacking force is then:
Pmax =
P
(0.85 ∗0.85 = 1097.89kN
(5.19)
The manufacturer [13], recommends limiting the maximum prestressing load to 70% of
the ultimate load, which is more restrictive than the limit imposed by the Eurocode [4].
Thus, the minimum ultimate load required is:
Pu,req = Pmax
0.7
= 1568.41kN
(5.20)
The number of strands required is chosen from the DYWIDAG catalogue:
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
39
==PAGE:52==
Figure 5.7: Ultimate load for strands in the ME coupler strands. Source: DYWIDAG [13].
It can be seen from Table 5.7 that the require number of strands providing sufficient ulti-
mate load is 6 strands of 15.7mm in diameter (Type 6806).
The duct diameter is 65mm. However, in order to decide the cross-section dimensions
of R4, the anchorage zone details must be consulted to provide with sufficient concrete
dimensions to embed the Coupler ME system. The minimum width of the ring to be able to
host the system is 220mm: 130mm for the system and 45mm of nominal concrete cover
to each side.
The height of the section is kept as 440mm. The prestress of this ring is a complicated step
in the construction process (Chapter 9) and because of this, it is beneficial to only have
only one tendon. This means the bottom element in TR3 will carry the prestress and ab-
sorb the tension forces from the cupula, with a cross section o 440mmx220mm. Whilst the
top element in the elements directly below the cupula will no longer receive the outwards
push and therefore will have the established minimum cross-section of 440mmx100mm.
The necessary block out dimensions to carry out the post-tensioning of the ring through
a void in the bottom beam of elements TR3 depends on the total elongation of the tendon
(see Figure 5.8).
Figure 5.8: Block-out dimensions in the Coupler ME system. Source: DYWIDAG [13].
∆Ltot = ∆Lp + ∆Lc + ∆Lsl + ∆Le
(5.21)
40

==PAGE:53==
Where ∆Lp is the elongation of the strand tendon, calculated as shown below, with Lp
being the length of the tendon in metres (2πR4 = 2π(10.5334m) = 66.18 m).
∆Lp =
P
ApEp
Lp =
793.23kN
(61
4π15.72)mm2 ∗195GPa ∗66.18m = 0.23m
(5.22)
∆Lc is the elastic deformation of the concrete:
∆Lc = σcm
Ec
Lc = 8.2MPa
195GPa66.18m = 0.0028m
(5.23)
With σcm being the average stress in the concrete cross section at the center of gravity
(NEd/(0.44*0.22)=8.2 MPa) and Lc is the length of the concrete member, or the circum-
ference length of the ring, which is 2πR4 = 2π(10.5334m) = 66.18 m.
∆Lsl sum of anchor plate impressions and wedge draw-in according to the anchorage/
coupling type applied, taken as 4mm for coupler ME, according to [13].
Finally, ∆Le is the elongation due to wedge draw-in, taken as 8mm also according to
[13] for ME couplers.
The total elongation is then:
∆Ltot = 0.23 + 0.0028 + 0.004 + 0.008 = 0.245m
(5.24)
By looking at Figure 5.8, the only missing parameter in calculation the block-out length is
s, which is defined as:
s = 0.2∆L
2
= 24.5mm
(5.25)
However this parameter must not be less that 120mm.
C is the length of the anchoring device, in this case, 120mm. This must be added to
the length ∆L+s+100+300 to find out the length of the block-out.
The result is a total length of 885mm is needed as space to carry out the post-tensioning
task inside the bottom beam of a TR3 element. This does not present a problem in the
design as the bottom beam of this element is 3.66m long and therefore, there is plenty of
space for the installation of this prestressing system.
Lastly, surface reinforcement will have to be included along the perimeter of the cross sec-
tion, according to Annex J in [4], to avoid spalling of the concrete as no mild reinforcement
has been included in the cross-section.

6
Design of radial arches
In the present chapter, the procedure leading to the final design of the radial arches is
described. Both the concrete section dimensions and the reinforcement are optimized to
hold the sectional forces resulting from the loads described in Chapter 4.
The vertical arch structures forming the dome can be divided into two categories because
of the radial symmetry in the structure:
• Arch type 1: spanning from the substructure to the middle top point of an edge arch.
• Arch type 2: spanning from edge arch to edge arch.
In the latter case, all of the arches span always from and to the same points in different
edge arches, which means the span is the same in all radial arches which are supported
solely on edge arches.
Figure 6.1: Highlighted examples of radial arches analyzed in 2D.
6.1
Sectional Forces in the radial arches
This section describes the procedure to obtain the sectional forces in the arches: from the
finite element model set-up to the load application and result extraction. The purpose of
this section is to obtain the sectional forces so they can later be used to design the arches
in Section 6.2.
6.1.1
Model description
A finite element model is set up, aided by Robot Structural Analysis software, to model
both arch types in 2D. The models were set up because it enables faster calculations of
sectional forces when dead load varies as the concrete cross section parameters change
during design optimization.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
43
==PAGE:56==
Three-hinged arch models were used as they result in larger design sectional forces than
two-hinged arch models. It is unknown how much bending moment can be taken by the
connection of the arch to the top ring and the latter to the rest of the arches so, in order
to design the cross section, it is on the safe side to assume no moment is taken on by
the rest of the structure and therefore, to use the sectional forces obtained from a three-
hinged arch model when designing.
Figure 6.2: 2D model of Arch Type 1
Figure 6.3: 2D model of Arch Type 2
The models were checked to be working correctly by making hand calculations for a simple
load case and comparing the results for bending moments and reactions. An example of
this check for Arch Type 1 can be found in Annex C.
6.1.2
Load Cases
The values of loads obtained in Chapter 4 are then applied to the model. Most of the
loads require transformations in order to apply them to the bars and nodes in the model.
A few require more than one load case to be set up to ensure the most critical sectional
forces for each section are found.
The following sections discuss the mentioned issues and explain the application of each
of the load types being considered, to the model.
44
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:57==
Structural dead load
The dead load calculation, as explained in Chapter 4, is broken down into the different el-
ements composing each trapezoidal element. Each of these loads is applied accordingly:
the load for edge beams and light concrete as trapezoidal distributed loads along the bars
and the loads representing the weight of the top and bottom beams at the nodes. This is
shown in Tables 6.1 and 6.2, where a trapezoidal element, Ti, corresponds to bar number
i. The weight share for each arch is assumed to be half of the weight of the trapezoidal el-
ements to each side, i.e. the weight of one trapezoidal element on each bar on the model.
An exception was made for the end elements in Arch Type 2, due to the irregular shapes
of T3SE, bar 3 in the model, and T11SE, bar 29 in the model (see Figure 2.4 for element
identification).
Figure 6.4: Weight share on half of an Arch type 2 and accountable
parts for point loads at the ends.
The weight of bar 3 in Arch Type 2 is calculated as holding the weight of a full T3 element
(whole weight share on one arch shown as blue contour in Figure 6.4, which is on the safe
side. Bar 29 collects the weight of a uniform load equal to the value of the trapezoidal
load at the bottom of T11. The point load at node 1, representing the weight of the bottom
beams of the trapezoidal elements, is calculated taking into account half of the length of
the T3 bottom beam plus half the bottom/inclined beam in T3SE (in red in Figure 6.4) .
At node 29, the load corresponds to half the length of the bottom beam in T3SE and the
whole length of the bottom beam in element T11SE (in green in Figure 6.4).
The resulting values can be observed in Figures 6.5 and 6.6.

Wind load
To find the critical moments for wind load, the two load cases shown in Figure 4.7 are
analyzed.
For easy identification, the load case where wind pressure occurs at the left side of the
arches (as shown in Figures 6.2 and 6.3) has been called “Left skew wind (WLskewL)”
and the load case where only wind suction occurs has been called “Right skew wind
(WLskewR)”.
The wind coefficients for both load cases are extracted from 4.7 and the product of these
coefficients and the peak velocity pressure, calculated in Section 4.2.2, is the surface load
acting upon each trapezoidal element.
The resultant load is obtained by multiplying by the area of each element and then, the
transformation in Equation 4.2 is used to find the trapezoidal loads representing the wind
load for each element.
The resultant of wind load on the lantern is divided by the number of top elements (18)
and this load is applied at nodes 7 and 8. The value applied is found at Tables 6.4 and
6.5 under the column for the resultant P.

Imposed load
As mentioned in Section 4.2.1, the imposed load Qk can be placed anywhere along the
arch and in order to find the load cases producing the most critical moments, all possible
load positions must be studied. This was set up in Robot by using a moving load along
52
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:65==
the bars and producing a load case every 0.2 m it advances.
The result is an envelope of all the load cases, which can be used in SLS and ULS combi-
nations along with other loads and will result in the most critical sectional forces for each
section of the arch.
6.1.3
Design sectional forces
The ULS and SLS combinations collected in Annex A are also implemented in Robot to
obtain envelopes for the sectional forces.
6.2
Design of the radial arches
In the present section, the procedure leading to the final section design of the radial arches
is described. Both the concrete section dimensions and the reinforcement are optimized
to hold the section forces in Section 6.1.
It must be noted that the total cross-sectional area of the arch is composed by two edge
beams from two adjacent trapezoidal elements placed together.
The process followed in the design for the arch is the following:
1. Pre-dimension the concrete section to obtain initial cross-section dimensions.
2. Design pre-stress and concrete cross-section to comply with stress limitations in
SLS.
3. Check stresses and optimize cross-section dimensions.
4. Update cross-sectional dimensions in structural self-weight calculation and re-calculate
sectional forces with new self-weight load.
5. Iterate steps 2 and 3 until the optimization of the cross-section and the pre-stress.
6. Check moment capacity in ULS.
7. If necessary, design mild reinforcement to achieve enough moment capacity.
The same procedure is used to design Arch Type 1 and Arch Type 2.
6.2.1
Arch Type 1
This section aims to give an overview of the steps in the process leading up to the final
dimensions and reinforcement of Arch Type 1.
Pre-dimensioning
Before designing the pre-stress, an initial calculation is carried out to obtain an initial width
and depth of the section. This is done by checking if the section is enough to comply with
stress limitations in SLS characteristic (CHR). The restrictions extracted from [4] Section
7.2 state that the compressive stress should be limited to:
σc ≤0.6fck = 33MPa
(6.2)
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
53
==PAGE:66==
As expected, if the stresses at the top and at the bottom fibre of the concrete section
are calculated, we can see some tension developing, as well as some large compression
stresses:
σc,top = −NEd
Ab
−MEd
Ib
zct
(6.3)
σc,bot = −NEd
Ab
−MEd
Ib
zcb
(6.4)
Where zct and zcb are the distances from the centroid to the top and bottom concrete
fibers, respectively and zcb is negative.
As compression stresses are larger than the limitation in Equation 6.2, the section di-
mensions need to be increased. The gross concrete cross-section properties are initially
taken as:
Ab = hb
(6.5)
Ib = 1
12bh3
(6.6)
It is logical to try to reduce
the compressive stresses
by increasing the section
depth, h, rather than the
width, because a small in-
crease will have a large
effect on the inertia, as
its value is raised to the
power of 3.
However,
for aesthetic purposes, it
is chosen that the depth
is the same all along the
arch and kept as slender
as possible.
Instead, the
width b is varied linearly
along the arch to optimize
the cross-section and try to
achieve a structure which
is as light as possible.
Figure 6.17: Close up of arch sections in 3D showing variation
in the width b along the arch segments.
For this reason, the cross-section dimensions are varied until the compressive stress
limitation is complied with, and with a variation of the width preferred over an increase in
depth, to a certain extent (b<1m).
Pre-stress and cross-section design
The tension stresses developing in the concrete also need to be dealt with. For this
purpose, it has been decided to pre-stress the arch so as to avoid cracks in SLS.
σc > fctk = −4.2MPa
(6.7)
The first assumption is to position the pre-stress in the centre of the concrete section.
Meaning that the distance from the centre of the pre-stress to the top concrete fibre (dp)
54
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:67==
is h/2.
dp = h
2mm
(6.8)
This is not the most effective way of pre-stressing the arch as there could be a much
smaller pre-stressing force acting at the required eccentricity for each section that it would
help compensate for the external bending moments. However, in order to compensate
bending moments the cable curve would have to have a negative eccentricity (cable below
the centroid). The reason for the decision to not take advantage of this eccentricity of the
pre-stress is that, during construction, when the trapezoidal elements are pre-stressed, no
other loading is acting on them other than self-weight and by introducing negative eccen-
tricities in the cables, tension may develop on the top fibers of the elements during transfer.
The resulting dimensions of the radial arches after the design process seem reasonable
even if there is no eccentricity given to the cable and therefore, this is the chosen solution.
The situation at the moment of transfer, SLS quasi-permanent and SLS characteristic
have all been investigated, but having no eccentricity of the cable means no moments
are induced during the pre-stressing and when the arches are lifted, when no loads or
only dead load applies. As consequence SLS characteristic combinations are the most
restrictive not only with respect to tension limitations but also, compression limitations and
therefore, the sectional forces resulting from SLS characteristic combinations are used in
the pre-stress design.

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway

all comply with the restrictions. However, the stresses may remain under the restrictive
values for these other sections even if the cross sectional dimensions are reduced.
As specified earlier, the depth of the section has been decided to be kept constant and
therefore, the width b is varied in each section to obtain the most optimized dimensions
without compromising the stress limitations.
The envelopes shown in the tables in Annex D show that the critical section at each bar
is usually at one of the end nodes of a bar, and therefore the widths have been chosen
to vary linearly from node to node, or from the top to the bottom of one same trapezoidal
element. In bar 3, the most critical section occurs halfway between the edge nodes and
because of this, node 3 and 4 are defined with the same width b, to guarantee enough
width at the critical point.
At this point, a minimum width has to be established so that the concrete cover is enough
around the pre-stress. A 12C15 tendon has a diameter ϕp=85mm as shown in Figure 6.18
and a concrete cover of at least one tendon diameter is demanded to be provided. A min-
imum width of 3 times the diameter, i.e. 255mm, is needed for each of the edge beams in
the adjacent trapezoidal elements, making a total of 510mm for the section width of the
arch.
This means a very large cross-section compared to the sectional forces in some of the
sections, especially the ones in the shorter span half-arch (right side in Figure 6.2). The
right and left sides of the arches will be pre-stressed separately as half-arches during con-
struction, so it was decided to design the pre-stress for these separately, allowing for a
lower number of strands, a lower required concrete cover and lower compression forces
in the concrete that will allow to reduce the concrete cross section significantly.
The pre-stress force used is 1690 kN, which is also the force used in Arch Type 2. This
force requires 8 strands to comply with the limitation in Equation 6.16 and this results in a
pre-stress area of Ap=1413.71mm. Again, this must be divided into two to apply 4C15 to
each edge beams of the trapezoidal elements composing the arch cross-section. These
tendons will have a diameter ϕp=50mm.
The section width needed to keep the concrete cover is more restrictive in this case than
the width needed to comply with the stress limitations, and therefore a width equal to 3
times the diameter, i.e., 150mm is needed for each of the edge beams in the adjacent
trapezoidal elements, making a total of 300mm width for the short half-arch in Arch Type
1.

Update cross-sectional dimensions in self-weight calculation
With the new dimensions for each cross-section, the self-weight calculations in Section
4.1.1 are updated and transferred to Robot to obtain some new sectional forces.
Iteration
The new sectional forces are used as input into the inequalities in Equations 6.9 to 6.12,
in order to re-design the cross-section dimensions and pre-stress. Then, new dimensions
for the widths at each section are found and again, new calculations for the self-weight are
required. This process continues until the sectional forces obtained from Robot derive in
cross-sectional dimensions and pre-stress which are the same as the previous iteration.

The next step in the design is to check the moment capacity in ULS.

Mild reinforcement
The result from the moment capacity calculations show no need for mild reinforcement
to contribute to the moment capacity as the design forces are already lower than the
capacity of the section. Therefore, according to [4] Section 7.3.2(4), there is no need to
provide the section with mild reinforcement. However minimum surface reinforcement will
be provided along the perimeter of the section to prevent spalling according Annex J in
[4].
6.2.2
Arch Type 2
Arch Type 2 is designed in the same way as Arch Type 1. The process is very similar and
therefore this section will only discuss the procedure briefly and highlight the differences
in the design of Arch Type 2 with respect to Arch Type 1.
Pre-dimensioning
The stress limitations in Equations 6.2 and 6.7 need to be respected in the design. The
initial cross-sectional dimensions with whom to begin the iteration process are the ones
keeping the top and bottom fibers in the concrete with a compressive stress of less than
0.6fck.
Pre-stress and cross-section design
Similarly to Arch Type 1, the pre-stress is not given any eccentricity to avoid tension devel-
oping during the pre-stressing and construction processes, and therefore, the sectional
forces extracted from the envelopes in the SLS characteristic combinations are the most
critical when it comes to stress limitations.
The critical sections with the maximum and minimum bending moment are shown in Table
6.10. However, all sections need to be checked to ensure these sections are, in fact, the
critical sections and that no section with bending moments with lower magnitude but also
lower compression, results in a worse case scenario.

6.3
Final design of the radial arches
The cross-section design of the radial arches consists of two edge beams from trapezoidal
elements placed together, adding up to a rectangular strong concrete section with a depth
of 440mm and a varying width along the arches. The width of the arches is displayed in
Figure 6.20.
Figure 6.20: Arch widths specified for each Arch Type. Dimensions in mm.
The section cuts for the trapezoidal elements marked in red can be found in Figure 6.22.
The tendon layout inside the arches is shown in Figure 6.21. All the half-arches will
have 2x4C15 except the longer spanned half-arch in Arch Type 1, which is provided with
2x12C15.
64
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:77==
Figure 6.21: Tendon layout in Arch Type 1 and Arch Type 2.
The fact that the arches are made up of two trapezoidal elements placed together and
their edge beams make up the section for the arch obligues to provide at least 2 equal
tendons, one per edge beam. During construction, the trapezoidal elements forming one
radial portion of the dome will be pre-stressed together before being lifted into position
next to each other and therefore, a tendon is needed on both edge beams of a trape-
zoidal element.
The edge beams in adjacent trapezoidal elements in one same arch have exactly the
same dimensions except at the longer span in Arch Type 2 (in green in Figure 6.20). It
has been chosen to endow the edge beam corresponding to the trapezoidal elements
which are also connected to Arch Type 1 (in blue) with a larger width than the one con-
nected to the short span in Arch Type 1 (yellow). This is due to two reasons:
• By doing this, the elements between the arch in yellow and the arch in magenta and
the elements between the arch in yellow and the arch in green will have the same
section dimensions. This means one less different type of element per ring layer,
which reduces production costs as the moulds used in making the precast elements
can be recycled.
• The other reason is that it is useful to give the edge beam of the elements also
connected to the blue arch a larger width. This is due to the pre-stress needing a
curvature to follow the shape of the skewed egde trapezoidal elements in the lower
part of the dome. This will be further discussed in Chapter 8.
The cross-section of the trapezoidal elements results as shown in the figures below. Note
that the sections shown correspond to the red cuts in Figure 6.20, and so the element
types shown are T4 and T11.
Figure 6.22: Cross-section of trapezoidal elements T4 and T11.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
65
==PAGE:78==
When the elements are placed together during construction, the sum of the adjacent edge
beams account for the section required for the radial arches. The section of the edge
beams in the trapezoidal elements of each ring layer will vary as the width along the
radial arches varies.

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:79==
7
Design of edge arches
This chapter is dedicated to the design of the three edge arches that support the radial
arches on each of the two dome structures. The main function of the edge arches is to
support the loads transferred by the radial arches, simply supported on them at points E1
to E5 (see Figure 7.2), although they will also have to withstand transverse wind loads on
the glass parapets and the weight of the glass cover on the gap between the domes.
Figure 7.1: Position of edge arches in the whole structure.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
67
==PAGE:80==
Figure 7.2: Points of load transfer from radial arches to edge arch.
An important characteristic of the edge arches is their inclination, as mentioned in Chapter
2. The arches are tilted inwards towards the centre of the dome, mainly due to aesthetic
and lighting reasons, but it also favours stability. The horizontal component of the loads
from radial arches which are not taken by the bottom ring of the cupula will work to push
the arches outwards, however this tilting of the arches contributes with a moment due to
its self-weight which acts to counteract these horizontal forces.
7.1
Sectional forces in the edge arches
The aim of this section is to obtain the sectional forces on the edge arches. All of them
are considered to have the same cross section and therefore, only one of the edge arches
will be considered in the design.
7.1.1
Model description
A 3D model of an edge arch is set up in Robot Structural Analysis. This is due mainly
to the fact that the edge arches will most likely behave as two-hinged arches and hand
calculations become complex. Also, similarly to radial arches, a certain degree of itera-
tions are needed to obtain the final section dimensions and creating a model facilitates
the repetitive process of updating the self-weight load.
The fact that a 3D model is needed in this case is due to the fact that the edge arches are
inclined. This means moments in z are no longer insignificant, but instead have a strong
influence in the design.
The arch is hinged at both ends in both y and z (local axes shown in Figure 7.3). Ex-
tra transverse supports are included at the points where the top and bottom beams of
68
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:81==
trapezoidal elements T1 and T2 connect one edge arch to the other. This will simulate
the interaction between the edge arches and will also indicate a portion of the compres-
sion force that these beams will experience in order to compensate the tension derived
from self-weight. More details on the design of these elements can be found in Chapter 8.
The definition of supports is shown in Figure 7.4, where f stands for ‘free´ and x stands for
‘fixed´ and the code is UxUyUzRxRyRz, representing the translations and rotations along
each axis.
Figure 7.3: Edge arch model - front and side view.
Figure 7.4: Edge arch model - 3D view.
7.1.2
Load Cases
The loads considered for the edge arch are: its self-weight, the point loads from the radial
arches, the weight of the glass in the space between domes and the transverse wind on
the inclined glass walls.
Self-weight
Unlike the radial arches, which have a complicated section to model due to the different
material involved, the edge arch is purely made out of strong concrete, and therefore the
section can be modelled in the FE software to account for self-weight automatically. The
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
69
==PAGE:82==
material properties described in Chapter 2 Section 2.5 are used as input.
Point loads from radial arches
From the results from Section 6.1, we can obtain the maximum and minimum values of
reactions at the ends of Arch Type 1 and Arch Type 2, or in other words, the loads trans-
ferred to the edge arches at points E1 to E5.
The vertical reactions for dead load only, obtained from the Arch Type 1 and Arch Type 2
models, are shown in Figures 7.5 and 7.6.
Figure 7.5: Vertical reactions from Arch Type 1 - dead load only.
Figure 7.6: Vertical reactions from Arch Type 2 - dead load only.

There will be several load cases to check, as the components of reactions due to dead
load will be permanent but the additional reaction values due to SLS and ULS combina-
tions will be able to occur in only a certain number of radial arches at a time. This means
the worst possible scenario has to be found in order to proceed in the design and there-
fore, a number of combinations have been set up where all points E1-E5 are loaded with
the dead load but only some may also be loaded with an additional force due to other
loads.
For this, aside from a load case with the dead load reactions, a load case is needed
for each point E with the value of the difference between the maximum value in SLS or
ULS and the dead load, i.e. the additional value due to other loads.
When these load cases have been set, all the possible combinations between loaded/non-
loaded points (with loads other than dead load) have to be evaluated to produce the most
critical moment, to be used in the design.

Weight from glass in the opening between domes
The weight from the glass is distributed along the length of the edge arches, and each
of the edge arches supporting the middle glass opening will take approximately half of its
weight. The load is higher at the top as the glass is wider, which increases the sagging
moment at centre span.
Although the edge arches in between domes (see Figure 7.1) may experience the weight
of some possible snow load on the glass, the skew snow load cases considered in Section
4.2.3, show that this load will have just the opposite effect on the arch than the glass load
and the point loads from the arches. The snow loads are higher towards the edges and
72
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:85==
as mentioned, the glass load reaches a maximum value at the crown of the arch. The
point loads from the radial arches are also mostly applied on the top part of the arch, this
means snow load will act favourably, reducing the peak sagging and hogging moments
and also increasing compression. Therefore, only the glass weight has been considered
in the combination with loads from the radial arches.
The weight of the glass transferred to one edge arch, varies from 0 at the supports to
a value of -2.14 kN/m at the top. The values along the arch are calculated as:
qglass,x = ρglass
w(x)
2
t
(7.1)
Where ρglass is the density of the glass (24.5 kN/m3), w(x) is the width of the glass at
position x, i.e., the distance between arches, and t is the thickness of the glass, assumed
to be 50mm.
The following figure shows the load case applied to the edge arch model:
Figure 7.11: Load case for the weight of the glass on one edge arch.
Wind load
The external edge arches will be affected by the wind load applied to the glass walls,
transferring the load onto the edge arch.
For each bar n, the resultant wind load applied is the equivalent to:
Pwind,n = qpAn
(7.2)
Where qp is the peak velocity pressure calculated in Section 4.2.2 and An is the surface
area of glass directly below the bar.
An = (hi2,n −hi1,n)wn

The inclined height hi1,n corresponds to the lowest point on the bar and hi2,n is the highest
point. As described in 2, the edge arches are inclined an angle of 12.67º to the vertical,
and so are the glass walls placed against them. Therefore, these inclined heights corre-
spond to the vertical height divided by the cosine of this angle. The horizontal distance
between the end nodes of a bar n is wn.
The resulting load is then transformed into trapezoidal loads along the bar by using Ex-
pression 4.2 and considering the similarity in the relationship between the heights of both
ends of the bar and the value of the wind load at these points:
h1,n

7.1.3
Design sectional forces
The arches in between the two domes, experiencing the weight of the glass of the glass
cover, will not experience transverse wind from the inclined glass walls. The other arches
will experience this wind load acting on the glass parapets but will not be subjected to the
load from the glass cover. This means these two load cases will not be used simultane-
ously in load combinations.
The ULS and SLS combinations used to find the most critical sectional forces can be
found in Annex A Table A.3. The dead load (including the point loads from radial arches)
is unfavourable and therefore affected by a factor of 1.1 in ULS combinations.
The combinations are not exactly true as the point loads from radial arches that are not
due to dead load are already factored, as the value used is extracted from the envelopes
from SLS and ULS combinations. When loads acting directly on the edge arch, like wind
load, are factored as a dominant load and combined with the values of the loads from the
radial arches, which are also already factored, it is likely that some combinations are com-
puting 2 dominant loads at a time. The forces obtained are higher than what is expected
in reality. This adds extra safety when it comes to design.
The first thing to acknowledge in order to begin the design of the edge arches, is the
fact that they are tilted inwards. They are to be pre-stressed as an arch on one plane be-
fore being lifted into their inclined position. Therefore, the arch will be subjected to skew
bending as the bending moments, obtained from the FE-model will not follow the direction
of the principal axes of the section. The sectional forces must then be resolved into the
two components along the principal centroidal axes (y and z in Figure 7.14), which are
tilted 12.67º to the local vertical and horizontal axes in the model (v and h in Figure 7.14),
which also match the direction of the global axes.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
75
==PAGE:88==
Figure 7.14: Section showing the principal axes of the section and the local axes in the model.
These envelopes for the resulting sectional forces in SLS characteristic and ULS are
shown in Figures 7.15 and 7.16:
Figure 7.15: SLS envelope of My and Mz for edge arches.
76
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:89==
Figure 7.16: ULS envelope of My and Mz for edge arches.
All the sections are considered in the design, meaning that all combinations are used in
order to find the maximum and minimum of each of the sectional forces for each of the
sections analysed. In this case, it has been decided to calculate sectional forces in 11
points along each bar in the model.
7.2
Edge arch design
The next sections use the bending moments along the principal axes for design, My and
Mz.
The procedure followed in the design is briefly summarized as:
1. The design of the cable shape considering dead load only.
2. Design of the prestressing force and cross-section dimensions to comply with stress
limitations in SLS.
3. Moment capacity analysis in ULS.
4. Design of mild reinforcement required.
7.2.1
Cable shape
The point loads from the radial arches are very unfavourable to the arch and as the shape
is inefficient to withstand the loads, it would result in a very bulk cross-section of the edge
arches in order to resist the sectional forces that would arise. For this reason, it has been
decided that the design of prestress in the arch should take advantage of cable eccen-
tricity to compensate these forces and achieve smaller section dimensions.
Only permanent loads (dead loads) are used in this step, although the dead load from
the edge arch’s self-weight has been ignored at this point as the dimensions of the cross
section are still unknown and the bending moments are fairly small. It is not surprising as
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
77
==PAGE:90==
the self-weight from the arch is a uniform load and the arch shape is very efficient in resist-
ing the forces from this type of loading, producing almost no bending moment. The small
bending moments that arise are due to the arch being divided into straight segments, the
Pearl-Chain elements, but these moments are small if compared to the bending moments
from the point loads (See Figures 7.17 and 7.18).
Figure 7.17: Moment diagrams for self-weight of the edge arch.
Figure 7.18: Moment diagrams for dead load from radial arches on edge arch.
The moment diagrams for both y and z, principal axes of the section, are obtained from
Robot:
78
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:91==
Figure 7.19: My diagram for dead load from radial arches on edge arch.
Figure 7.20: Mz diagram for dead load from radial arches on edge arch.
The cable must follow a parabolic shape that reflects the shape of the moment diagram
for dead load. In this case, the moment diagrams are symmetric, and therefore so will the
cable shape, so only half of the span is analyzed.
The cable path in the XZ plane, compensating My is divided into 3 parabolic sections
for half of the span, whilst the eccentricities in plane XY, compensating Mz moments, is
divided into 5 parabolic sections.
The parabola equations are chosen to maximise the compensation of the bending mo-
ments and therefore, the parameters defining the shape are chosen to match the shape
of the moment diagram, as shown in Figures 7.22 and 7.23.
The parameters used are shown in Figure 7.21, where half of the span is shown for an
equivalent beam with a cable shape defined by 3 parabolas. These define all the param-
eters used in the description of the parabola shapes.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway

==PAGE:92==
Figure 7.21: Parameters defining the cable path
The parameters describing the parabolic sections are:
• e0B, eccentricity of the ducts at the end section.
• βe0B, eccentricity of the ducts at the initial section.
• β1e0B, eccentricity of the ducts at the points with maximum or minimum moment.
• l, length of half of the section being divided into parabolas.
• αl, distance to the point of maximum or minimum moment along the distance l.
• α1l, distance from the end to the point of inflection with the previous parabola.
