7.2.2
Prestress and cross-section design
The prestress is designed by restricting the stresses in the section in SLS:
σc ≤0.6fck = 33MPa
(7.24)
σc ≥fctk = −4.2MPa
(7.25)
The situation at the moment of transfer, where the arch holds no other loads needs to be
investigated to avoid cracks in the concrete before it is placed in position. When introduc-
ing eccentricities, the prestress induces bending moments that may result critical if the
section is not sufficient. SLS characteristic is also investigated but results less critical.
The interaction between moments in both axes requires the check of the stresses in all 4
extreme concrete fibers, located at the corners of the rectangular section. For reference,
the stresses at these points have been named as shown in Figure 7.24, where ”top/bot”
means at the top or bottom of the concrete section,”in/out” means to the inner or external
side of the inclined section (i.e., towards the rest of the structure or facing the outside).

Update self-weight calculation and iteration
As mentioned earlier, the self-weight of the arch produces bending moments of very low
values and therefore, the changes in the self-weight do not affect the stress calculations
significantly. However, a few iterations are still needed to ensure the sectional forces used
are the corresponding to the cross section being analysed. This update of the self-weight
is carried out by inserting the new section dimensions into the FE-model.
Moment capacity in ULS
The next step in the design is to check the moment capacity in ULS. The same calculation
procedure as for the radial arches is followed (See Chapter 6), with some variations.
The first difference in the approach is that the edge arches are subjected to biaxial bend-
ing. The moment capacity of the section is then calculated for each of the axes separately
and their interaction will be addressed subsequently.
86
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:99==

tions are used in the capacity calculations.

Mild reinforcement
The interaction expression in Equation 7.46 is used to calculate the mild reinforcement
needed. The approach consists of a trial and error procedure, by assuming the diameter
of the bars and calculation how many are needed to comply with Equation 7.46.

ds(My) = h −cnom,s −ϕs/2
(7.47)

Ts = Asfyk/γs

Top and bottom edge beams in elements T1 and T2

elements in the finite element model used for the analysis of the edge arch. The supports
are shown in Figure 8.1, with the values of the reactions in SLS and ULS.

beam (Bb).

breq = 0.12m

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:105==
The requires section width in ULS is 0.10m, which is less restrictive than in SLS.
A minimum thickness of 100 mm is defined for all the perimeter beams in trapezoidal
elements and as the beam being analyzed is composed of the bottom beam in T3 plus
the top element in T2, the final chosen width for the beam is 200mm.
8.1.2
Design of beams Bb
The tension developing in the beams is fairly low and therefore, an analysis is carried out
to find out whether the force in can be resisted by the concrete itself without any reinforce-
ment, with relatively small cross-section dimensions.
In this case the stress limitation is:
σ = NBt
hb ≥fctk = −4.2MPa
(8.11)
and the required cross-section is 0.037 m in SLS and 0.05m in ULS. These dimensions
are very small and therefore it can be concluded that the minimum width of 200mm will
be enough. And as for the radial arches and edge arches, surface reinforcement must be
provided for extra safety in all edge beams.
However, a more detailed analysis of these elements is recommended in future calcu-
lations to evaluate the appearance of bending moments which can result very critical and
require reinforcement in these beam elements.
8.2
Shear in the light concrete filling
A simple verification of the shear resistance of the light concrete filling is carried out to
check that the thickness of the filling used is a realistic value given the loads that will act
on them. The thickness used in the design is 200mm.
Section 11.6 in [3] is used, stating that the design value of the shear resistance of a
lightweight concrete member without shear reinforcement VlRd,c is calculated as:
VlRd,c = [ClRd,cη1k(100ρlflck)0.33 + k1σcp]bwd ≥(η1vl,min + k1σcp)bwd
(8.12)
Where the recommended values for ClRd,c, vl,min and k1 are 0.15/γc, 0.0128k3/2f1/2
ck and
0.15, respectively. The coefficient η1 is found as:
η1 = 0.4 + 0.6ρ/2200 = 0.673
(8.13)
Where ρ is the upper limit of the oven dry density for the relevant class in accordance
with Table 11.1 in [4] and taken as 1000 kg/m3, corresponding to the lowest density class
collected in the Eurocode.
σcp is the mean compressive stress in the section which acts favourably in the shear
resistance and therefore is taken as 0 in this calculation.
Parameter k is calculated as:
k = 1 + d/200 = 2
(8.14)
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
93
==PAGE:106==
Where d is the depth of the cross-section, in this case 200mm. Finally, bw is the width of
the section, which varies along the trapezoidal elements. A very conservative verification
is done by checking if the narrowest section (top of T6), with a width of around 600mm
can take the maximum shear force ocurring along Arch Type 1, which is 100.62kN (see
Figure 8.4, showing the envelope of shear in ULS for Arch Type 1).
Figure 8.4: Envelope of shear forces in ULS for Arch Type 1.
The resulting shear resistance of the section, as defined by Equation 8.12, is 153.22
kN, which is 34% larger than the design shear force, and therefore it is same to assume
the 200mm thickness of the concrete filling is enough. Possibly, this thickness could be
reduced in future developments to reduce the weight of the structure even further.
8.3
Foundations
The design of foundations is beyond the scope of this project, however important horizon-
tal forces due to the inclination of the arches at their base will be transferred to foundations.
Bending moments will not be significant as concrete hinges will be casted at the connec-
tion between the arches and the substructure.
As a possible solution to avoid the transfer of these horizontal forces to the foundations,
is to connect the ends of the arches with tension rods that absorb these horizontal forces
and avoid the need to over-dimension the foundations. These rods can be embedded in
the structure below the dome, covering the freeway.
8.4
Connections and details
Connection details are also not part of the objectives of this report, however a few detail
connections suggestions are collected below for the sake of aiding the further develop-
ment of the project.
94
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:107==
8.5
Hinges
Concrete hinges are needed at the base of the arches which are supported on foundations
to avoid moment transfer. Philip S. Halding proposes a concrete hinge in [16], shown in
Figure 8.5.
Figure 8.5: Concrete hinge with reduced height at the hinge throat. Source: [16].
This solution consists of a narrowing of the concrete section, reinforced by crossing steel
bars that allows a certain degree of rotation in the perpendicular direction to the narrowing
of the section, therefore, reducing the moment transfer to the substructure. A modifica-
tion of this hinge is needed for the the ends of the edge arches, which need to be hinged
in both axes of their cross section. The analysis of a hinge with a narrowed section all
around, allowing bi-axial rotations is proposed as a topic for further investigation.
The hinge is not made to work in shear and therefore, to avoid shear from developing,
the hinge must be placed normal to the longitudinal axis of the last Pearl-Chain element
of the arch. A wedge-type element can be casted out to position the hinge in this normal
position. This is shown in 8.6.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
95
==PAGE:108==
Figure 8.6: Positioning of the concrete in a perpendicular position to the beam axis.

Joints
The connection between the different elementary Pearl-Chain elements composing the
dome is suggested to be done on site, with a precasted indentation in both adjacent ele-
ments that will be casted out as a whole unit after the assembling of all of the elements.
This has been inspired by [16] and an illustration of a possible joint between trapezoidal
elements is shown below:
Figure 8.7: In-situ casted joint between Pearl-Chain elementary units.

8.7

the concrete.

9
Construction process
The following chapter proposes a possible construction process for the dome. The most
important characteristic of the constructive process is the use of Pearl-Chain technology,
which allows a very efficient construction with regards to time consumption. The step by
step process of this proposal will be described in an ordered timeline.
9.1
Foundation
The first task is to complete the works on the foundation. The design of the substructure
is beyond the scope of this study and therefore, it will not be described further than by
saying that they will need dowel bars embedded into the substructure ready to connect
the edge arches. The foundation needed for a temporary steel pole to hold the radial
Pearl-Chain portions during construction will also be completed at this stage. In the case
where the ramming of the pole into the ground at a certain length so that the reaction from
the soil is enough to compensate the bending moment at the base, this task will also be
carried out at this stage, with the pole position being in the centre of the dome.
9.2
Post-tensioning and erection of the edge arches
The Pearl-Chain pieces of the edge arches are assembled on site on the ground (arch
lying flat against the surface) and the 7C15 cable is threaded through them. The jacking
process begins to post-tension the elements together.
The position of the arches on the ground must be trying to minimize the necessary ad-
justments with the lifting crane. The edge arches are the heaviest elements that will be
listed by the crane, weighing 340 kN, therefore the crane to be used must have a capacity
of 35T or more. The arches will be lifted from the points of maximum hogging moment,
which produces a situation with a similar bending moment diagram to the envelopes used
in its design. This load case must be investigated to check the design cross-section of
the arch is enough to resist this transient situation, which is not included in the objectives
of this work.
Once lifted into their inclined position, still being held by the crane, temporary vertical
steel supports will be provided to hold them in place, using wedges to adjust to the in-
clined rectangular cross-section.
When the edge arches are secured into position, the connection to the substructure is
made, by the casting of an in-situ concrete hinge, described in Chapter 8. When all 6
hinges have hardened, the edge arches are ready to receive the trapezoidal elements.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
99
==PAGE:112==
9.3
Post-tensioning and erection of the Pearl-Chain radial
portions of the dome
This step can really be carried out simultaneously with the last once the edge arches have
been pre-stressed, so the jacks can be used in post-tensioning the radial portions.
The trapezoidal elements composing each of the Pearl-Chain units of the dome are layed
out and the prestressing tendons are threaded through the lateral edge beams on each
side of the elements. The elements are prestressed together, ready to be lifted. Special
attention is drawn to the longer Pearl-Chain elements formed by elements T1 to T6. In
these portions of the dome, the prestress changes direction along with the skew edges of
the trapezoidal elements (see Figure 8.3). The component of the prestressing force acting
in the inwards direction because of this angle change is used to ensure compression in
the horizontal beams at this point. Therefore, the prestressing of these elements should
be carried out against a surface that can create a reaction that will compress these beams.
These longer portions are the first to be lifted by a crane and anchored with cables to
the steel pole at the top end. The reason why they should be lifted first is that the po-
sitioning of the portions which are supported on the edge arch will cause the edge arch
to move inwards, compressing the elements between the arches at the legs. Once an-
chored to the pole, the hinge at the supports connecting the arch composed by the edge
beams of the elements in contact in both adjacent longer radial portions, is casted out,
connecting the radial arch to the support.
All of the elements are lifted and anchored to the pole alternatively, i.e., when one of
the Pearl-Chain portions is held up by the pole, the next portion to be lifted is the one
directly opposite, in order to compensate the bending moments in the pole. This can re-
duce material consumption as a lighter profile may be used if the process is adjusted in
this way. The lifting and anchoring of the radial portions continues until the last one is
positioned on the edge arch and anchored to the pole.
9.4
Post-tensioning of the bottom ring
A side lifting equipment or structure is needed to support the jacking equipment next to
the trapezoidal element with the void in its lower edge beam.
The tendon is threaded around the ring until the first inserted end comes out from the
other side of the void, then both ends are threaded through the ME Coupler [13]. One of
the ends is anchored to the anchoring device with the use of a wedge and then jacking
begins from the other end, creating a belt-bucle effect and compressing the ring, bringing
all of the radial portions together.
Once the ring has been prestressed, the other end of the tendon is anchored in the same
way as the first and the whole system is embedded in the void which will be casted with
55MPa concrete.
100
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:113==
9.5
Joint casting
At this point, the elements are in their final position and the joints between the elements
can be casted out with mortar. The design of the joints is not part of the scope of this
project and therefore, they are not considered in the present report.
9.6
Temporary supports removal
Once the joints have hardened, at this point it is safe to remove the temporary supports
in the edge arches. This is done as symmetrically as possible to avoid skew loading on
the arch.
9.7
Finishings
The last steps in the construction process involve the installation of all glass surfaces and
the installation of the green roof, which will not be detailed in this project.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
101
==PAGE:114==
102
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:115==
10
Sustainability
The purpose of this chapter is to evaluate the sustainability of the dome design by cal-
culating the CO2 emmissions derived from its construction and establish a comparison
between the emmissions of an analogous in-situ casted concrete shell with the most com-
mon construction process used for this kind of structure.
According to [17], the CO2 emmissions of a building must consider all life cycle phases,
including: Production, building, use and end of life. The present chapter aims to estab-
lish the differences in CO2 production from the first two phases: production and building,
focusing mainly on material supply.
The data on CO2 emmissions per material used are extracted from [Co2kristian] and
summarized in Table 10.1.

At the production stage, the material consumption from the moulds used in casting the pre-

hmould is the required height of the mould walls, taken as 50cm.
The design has been influenced to reduce material waste and therefore the same trape-
zoidal element types are repeated as much as possible. The total element types is 11,
so only 11 moulds are needed and these are recycled to produce all of the trapezoidal
elements composing the dome.
The Pearl-Chain elements composing the edge arches also need to be casted out, the
largest of them being 5.59m long. The edge arches are symmetric and all three of them
are equal, so only 4 moulds are needed, corresponding to the 4 elements in one half-arch.
The volume needed for one edge arch element mould is:
Vmould,edgearch = 3(5.58 ∗0.62 ∗0.05) + 2(0.62 ∗0.62 ∗0.05) = 0.56m3
(10.3)

10.2
Emmissions in the construction phase
The Pearl-Chain technology in this design makes the construction process much more
efficient, both in material and time consumption, than traditional methods. The traditional
construction method includes temporary supports and formworks, usually made from tim-
ber, that have to hold the weight of the concrete as it hardens. The construction method
proposed in Chapter 9, uses a temporary steel pole that will support the radial portions
of the dome during construction. This section provides a rough estimation of the CO2
emmissions from the construction process in the design and the analogous structures by
calculating the material production necessary only in the construction phase.
The first step is estimating the material quantities used in formworks and the steel pole.
Analogous dome structure
The formwork needs a mould structure under all the concrete surface, but also vertical
supports holding this at the right height at each point. A mean height of 5m is used for the
supports, with a 15x15cm section. The compressive strength of CLT is estimated to be
around 38 MPa [19], and consequently, each one of them will hold up to maximum 855
kN of weight. The weight of the concrete surface plus the mould structure is:
Wc+f == 5115.95kN + 1744.16m2 ∗0.05m ∗4.7kN/m3 = 5525.83kN
(10.9)
So around 7 supports are needed for the dome, plus a 20% of their weight in added
bracings and stiffeners, the total estimated quantity of CLT for the formwork is:
Wtotal,f == 414kN = 42234.61kg
(10.10)
And the emmissions from its production are:
COformwork
2
= 0.51 ∗42234.61 = 21539.65kg
(10.11)
Pearl-Chain design
This value is to be compared with the production of a steel pole, which will anchor ca-
bles supporting the radial Pearl-Chain elements. The maximum flexural moment on the
pole can come from holding the largest of these radial portions (composed of T1 to T6)
with no other symmetric element compensating this bending. Larger bending on the pole
comes from a larger horizontal component of tension in the cable connecting the element
to the pole. The pole is assumed to be 12m high and the foundation starts at the bottom
of the freeway under the dome, around 6m under, so an 18m pole is used. The height
of the radial portion is 10m, therefore placed 2m away from the to of the pole horizontally
and vertically. The weight of the Pearl-Chain element is shared between the foundation
and the pole, assuming a 1:1 share, the weight taken by the pole is:
W0.5PCelement = 238.58 ∗0.5 = 119.29kN
(10.12)
Half of the sum of the weights of T1 to T6 (see Table 10.2).
The maximum moment produced in the pole (at its base) is:
Mpole = 119.29kN ∗2m + 119.29kN ∗18m = 2358.8kNm
(10.13)
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
105
==PAGE:118==
The design elastic bending moment resistance of the cross-section is:
MRd = Welfy/M0
(10.14)
And therefore the circular hollow cross section of the pole able to resist this moment is
a CHS 610/12.5. This profile can resist an axial force equal to around 2 times the total
weight of the dome structure, so this is the profile chosen for material estimation.
An extra 15% steel weight is added to account for the cable production and an extra
6m is added to the length of the pole to account for the required embedment length. The
total weight of steel is:
Wsteel = 184.2kg/m ∗24m ∗1.15 = 5304.96kg
(10.15)
With an inherent C02 production of:
COpolesystem
2
= 1 ∗5304.96 = 5304.96kg
(10.16)
CO2 emmissions comparison in the construction stage
The reduction in CO2 emmissions in this design compared to the most commonly em-
ployed construction methods is around 75%, which is a very significant reduction.

Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:119==
11
Conclusions and further development
The main objectives of this thesis were met, leading to a superstructure design that
demonstrates the viable application of Super-Light and Pearl-Chain technology to a geo-
metrically challenging concrete dome. The design is optimized towards weight alleviation,
providing lighter elements that do not only result visually appealing but also enable a sig-
nificant reduction in material consumption.
The result of this thesis also represents a valid solution to the desires of the Bispebjerg
Local Committee of creating an urban space with a green motif, including the creation of
a market space and a future landmark that symbolises a green gate to the city of Copen-
hagen, both combined as motivation for this thesis.
Firstly, the geometrical shape of the structure has been chosen to constitute an interest-
ing and aesthetically pleasing structure, but without compromising its integration into the
surroundings. The efficiency of the structural elements in carrying the load has also been
considered to some extent, but without limiting the freedom in the geometrical design. A
suggestion for further work on this topic is to investigate the effects of shape optimization
in the dome and evaluate other shapes other than the spherical, regarding the inherent
complications in the construction phase and also, the effects on material consumption in
the production of the Pearl-Chain elements that could form the optimized shape.
The basic load paths along the structure are described and used to propose a struc-
tural analysis approach in order to design the main elements of the structure: the cupula
rings, the radial arches and the edge arches. The sectional forces due to the loads are as-
sumed to be carried fully by the stronger concrete elements in the Pearl-Chain elements,
assuming no contribution to the resistance from the light aggregate concrete filling. More
detailed calculation can assess the effects of the contribution of the filling in the resistance
of the sections and the possibility to lighten the structure further.
The main loads on the structure are used in the design, skew load cases of variable
loads are chosen as they result more critical to the type of structure in this study. Load
combinations in Serviceability Limit State and in Ultimate Limit State are employed with
the aim of obtaining the axial forces and bending moments that are used in the design for
each of the main elements of the structure.
The cupula rings are pre-dimensioned by estimating the axial forces, providing the nec-
essary concrete section to withstand compression forces and the necessary prestress in
the bottom ring to prevent cracking. A specific post-tensioning system is proposed for the
bottom cupula ring and the cross-section dimensions of the ring are adjusted to be able
to host the system. Further calculations in this area may be directed towards obtaining
the bending moments in the edge beams composing the bottom ring due to the real dis-
tribution of the loads on the ring, where most of the load is applied at the connections with
the radial arches and also, the possible asymmetries in the loading. A possible solution
to the appearance of these moments is suggested in the relevant chapter.
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
107
==PAGE:120==
The radial arch-type structures composed by the edge beams in the trapezoidal elements
are analysed as three-hinged arches and the section is optimized by adjusting the width
of the edge beams, which varies linearly in the elements to adapt to the requirements
in the cross section to withstand the sectional forces in SLS and ULS limit states. Pre-
stress is provided to achieve lighter elements, in this case, positioned with no excentricity
to avoid inducing bending moments in the elements during jacking. The prestress also
serves to connect the Pearl-Chain elements in radial portions that permit the construction
process proposed. The design proposed for the edge arches includes the optimization of
the section by including a prestressing tendon with eccentricities with respect to both of
the principal axes of the cross-section. The cable shape is optimized to compensate the
bending moments from the weight of the radial arches supported on the arch. This design
allowed to reduce the concrete cross-section and therefore, lighten the structure, saving
material.
Simple verifications are carried out for the other elements of the structure, such as the
top and bottom beams of the lower trapezoidal elements connecting the edge arches to
one another, and also, a shear verification of the light aggregate concrete filling of the
elementary Pearl-Chain elements. This is to provide a starting point for further analysis
of these elements and give an estimation of their dimensions to analyse the total weight
of the structure and its consequent sustainability based on CO2 emmissions.
The design of connections, concrete hinges and foundations was out of the scope of
this project but some suggestions were made to guide the first steps of their future devel-
opment.
A general overwiew of a construction process proposal that may be applied to this de-
sign is proposed and broken down into sequential steps, which include the suggestion of
temporary structures that should complement the constructive process. The constructive
viability of the design and the technologies employed is proven with the proposal of a
valid construction process, which will also provide a faster completion of the construction
phase as compared to the traditional construction methods used in dome structures.
Finally, an analysis of the CO2 emmissions in the production and construction of the de-
sign proposed are carried out and compared to an estimation of the CO2 emmissions of
an analogous in-situ casted dome with the same spherical radius. The results show a
fairly low reduction of the emmissions during the production stage but a very significant
(75%) reduction of the emmissions during the construction phase. Conclusively, the de-
sign proposed in this thesis provides a more sustainable alternative to the most common
designs for concrete domes utilized nowadays.

==PAGE:134==
C
Annex
C.1
Robot model check
The model for Arch Type 1 is checked by evaluating the results obtained for a simple load
case both with the Robot model and with hand calculations.
The load case chosen to compare the results obtained from the Robot model and the
hand calculations is a point load of 7kN applied at position x=6.5m, which is close to the
position producing maximum moment.
The results from the FE-model are shown in Figure C.1.
Figure C.1: Moment diagram and reactions for a 7kN point load in Robot.
The dimensions needed for the hand calculations are:
• dA, horizontal distance from support A to centre hinge (21.19 m).
• dB, horizontal distance from support B, at the edge arch, to centre hinge (10.55 m).
• h, height of the highest point of the arch, in this case, 10 m.
• h2 , height of support B from the ground (7.87 m).
• x, horizontal distance of the load application point from support A (6.5 m).
• R, radius of the arch, which is 27.76 m.
• b, vertical height of the application point at x, 5.79 m, checked graphically as follows.
If the equation of the arch is:
(x −x0)2 + (y −y0)2 = R2
(C.1)
122
Design of a concrete construction to cover the Elsinore freeway
==PAGE:135==
with (x0,y0) being the centre of the arch. If the origin (0,0) is placed at support A, then
the centre of the arch has coordinates (dA, R-h), and therefore, the equation of the arch
becomes:
(x −dA)2 + (y −(R −h))2 = R2
(C.2)
By rearranging to find the y coordinate corresponding to x=6.5 m, height b is found.
b =
√
R2 −(x −dA)2 −R + h = 5.7946m
(C.3)
The horizontal and vertical reactions in both supports (HA, HB, RA and RB) are found by
equilibrium. By taking moments at the central hinge:
∑
M = −RAdA + HAh + Qk(dA −x) = 0
(C.4)
From this, HA can be found in terms of RA.
HA = RAdA −Qk(dA −x)
h
(C.5)
The horizontal equilibrium of forces allows to express HB in terms of RA:
∑
Fh = HA −HB = 0
(C.6)
HB = RAdA −Qk(dA −x)
h
(C.7)
And by the vertical equilibrium of forces it is possible to find RB in terms of RA:
∑
Fh = RA + RB −Qk = 0
(C.8)
RB = Qk −RA
(C.9)
All reactions are now expressed in terms of the vertical reaction in A, RA, so if moments
are taken at support B, then:
∑
MB = −RA(dA + dB) −HAh2 + Qk(dA + dB −x) = 0
(C.10)
and substituting Equation C.5 into Equation C.10, gives:
RA(dA + dB) −RAdA −Qk(dA −x)
h
h2 + Qk(dA + dB −x) = 0
(C.11)
RA = Qk(dA + dB −x −(dA−x)h2
h
)
dB + dA(1 −h2
h )
(C.12)
When Qk=7 kN, dA=21.19 m, dB=10.55 m, x=6.5 m, h2=7.87 m and h=10 m, then:
RA = 6.357kN
(C.13)
From Equations C.5, C.7 and C.9, we can obtain the other reactions:
HA = 3.187kN
(C.14)
HB = 3.187kN
(C.15)
RB = 0.643kN
(C.16)
It can be observed from Figure C.1, that the reactions obtained from Robot differ in less
than 1% from those obtained from hand calculations. Furthermore, if the moment at x is
calculated:
Mx=6.5 = RAx −HAb = 22.852kNm
(C.17)
Value which deviates only slightly (1.22%) from the value obtained in the FE-model, so
it is concluded that the model is correctly calculating reactions and sectional forces and
these can be used in the cross-sectional design.
